(Paper)
<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2046-2069/2011">RSC Adv.</a></span>, 2019, <strong>9</strong>, 18451-18458</div><h1 id="sect184"><span class="title_heading">Plasma treated TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers as high performance anode materials for sodium-ion batteries<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
<span class="bold">Rui Wang</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Rui&nbsp;Wang (orcid.org/0000-0001-5403-1628) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-5403-1628"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*, 
<span class="bold">Shuimei Chen</span>*, 
<span class="bold">Daming Ren</span>, 
<span class="bold">Songting Liu</span>, 
<span class="bold">Beibei He</span>, 
<span class="bold">Yansheng Gong</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Yansheng&nbsp;Gong (orcid.org/0000-0001-8197-9481) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8197-9481"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span><span class="bold"> and </span>
<span class="bold">Huanwen Wang</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Huanwen&nbsp;Wang (orcid.org/0000-0001-9880-7723) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-9880-7723"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>
<br><span class="italic">Faculty of Materials Science and Chemistry, China University of Geosciences, Wuhan 430074, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:wangrui@cug.edu.cn">wangrui@cug.edu.cn</a>; <a href="mailto:chensm920420@cug.edu.cn">chensm920420@cug.edu.cn</a></span>
</p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
30th April 2019
</span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 4th June 2019</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 11th June 2019</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> has been a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries because it is low-cost and environment-friendly. However, its electrochemical performance at high rates is still not acceptable. Herein, we synthesized a TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofiber material by the electrospinning method, and introduced air plasma treatments to modify the obtained material. Characterization results indicate that after the plasma treatments, the C fibers may have reacted with the plasma, and the surface areas of the nanofibers are increased. Electrochemical tests show this plasma treatment may be beneficial to the rate performance. The TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofiber with plasma treatment could deliver a high redox capacity of 191&nbsp;mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 500 cycles at a very high rate of 10C (3300 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). The superior effects of the plasma treatment on the rate performance may provide new insights for developing better materials for practical sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><hr>
<h2 id="sect247"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
<span>With the rapid development of consumer electronics and electrical vehicles, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely researched and are in frequent use nowadays.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1–7</span></sup></a> However, the potential limit and high cost of lithium resources are still big concerns, which may hinder the applications of LIBs in large-scale energy storage systems. Recently, with the advantage of low-cost and widespread availability of sodium resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have become an important and competitive option for electrical storage applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8–14</span></sup></a> However, in SIBs, since the radius of the Na ion is 43% larger than that of the Li ion, it is more difficult to find suitable electrode materials to accommodate sodium ions and achieve rapid and reversible ion insertion/extraction. Recently, many efforts have been made to find suitable anode materials for SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15–17</span></sup></a> As to the carbon based materials, most of them have irreversible reactions with sodium.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> Only a few hard carbons were reported to have attractive sodium storage performance, but their low potential and large polarization are still big concerns for battery applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19,20</span></sup></a> The alloying anodes such as Sb, Sn, and P, are other types of anode candidates and have shown high reversible capacities.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21–23</span></sup></a> However, the large volume change during cycles is a big obstacle, which leads to electrode pulverization, capacity loss and poor cycling performance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> Therefore, searching for low cost, reasonable performance anodes is still a great challenge in SIBs.</span>
<p class="otherpara">TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> is a low-cost, abundant, and environment-friendly material, which has shown large reversible capacity and good cyclability in LIBs. Recently, it is also proposed to be used as anode materials in SIBs, but the insulating nature of TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> is still a big problem, which may result in a poor electrochemical performance, especially the rate performance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> has many structures, and the sodium storage capabilities of polymorphs such as amorphous,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> anatase,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> and rutile<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28,29</span></sup></a> have been studied. Though their performances exhibit a large diversity, most of them deliver capacities lower than 200 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, only a few of them can deliver a capacity higher than 250 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (about 0.76C). In order to improve the rate performance, previous reports have suggested to make one-dimensional nanosized materials. Xiong <span class="italic">et al.</span> developed an electrospinning technique to prepare TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers, and the material can deliver a capacity of 164.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at a current density of 2000 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> Ge <span class="italic">et al.</span> prepared TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>@CNF composites at different temperatures, and their results indicated the material prepared at 550 °C presented higher capacity retention, while the material prepared at 650 °C possessed better rate capability.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> Wu and coworkers introduced nitrogen doping to the electrospun TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers, and improved the capacity from 87 to 108 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the rate of 10C.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a> These results suggest that electrospinning is an effective method to make one dimensional TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C fibers with improved performances.</p>
<p class="otherpara">Recently, the plasma-based technique has been applied to modify the surface of various materials. It has several advantages such as effective, pollution-free, uniform, and reproducible.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33,34</span></sup></a> In particular, if carbon-based material is treated and oxygen is involved as a working gas, the surface will be etched and become rougher.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> Liu <span class="italic">et al.</span> reported that in addition to etching the surface of the material, the biggest feature of plasma process is the generation of new structures, including edge-dangling bonds, defects and oxygen-containing functional groups, which may contribute to the conductivity of carbon materials.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> Lu <span class="italic">et al.</span> mentioned that oxygen-containing functional groups, such as C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O and C–O, played an important role in catalytic activity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a> In this case, it is interesting to modify materials with this plasma technique and observe their electrochemical performances. In this work, we used the electrospinning method to prepare TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers. Then the air plasma treatment was employed to modify the fibers and its effect on the electrochemical properties was investigated in details.</p>

<h2 id="sect288"><span class="a_heading">2. Experimental</span></h2>
<h3 id="sect291"><span class="b_heading">2.1 Preparation of materials</span></h3>
<span>TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers were prepared by the electrospinning technique with different precursor solutions. For TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers, 0.75 g polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP, <span class="italic">M</span><small><sub>w</sub></small> = 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">300<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">000) was dissolved in 10 mL ethanol (≥99.9%) at vigorous stirring for 6 h, then a mixture of 2 mL tetrabutyltitanate (Ti(OC<small><sub>4</sub></small>H<small><sub>9</sub></small>)<small><sub>4</sub></small>) and 2 mL acetic acid was dripped in. After stirring for 3 h, the obtained solution was used as the precursor solution.</span>
<p class="otherpara">In the electrospinning experiment, the precursor solution was loaded into a 10 mL syringe and the flow rate of solution was set to 9 mL h<small><sup>−1</sup></small> by a syringe pump. A high-voltage power supply was connected to the needle in order to apply a positive voltage of 12 kV and negative voltage of 2.5 kV between the needle and the collector for the formation of nanofibers. As prepared nanofibers were first heated in a muffle furnace at 200 °C in air for 2 h at a heating rate of 0.5 °C min<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, and then calcined in a tube furnace at 550 °C in Ar atmosphere for 3 h. Finally, a film composed of nanofibers was obtained, and is marked as TC.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The plasma treatment was carried on in a plasma equipment (PDC-MG, Mingheng, China). In a typical treating process, the obtained film was fixed on a glass plate in the chamber. The chamber was degassed to 100 Pa and then dry air was led into the reactor. The experimental power input from RF generator was 130 W and the treatment time is 3 min for each side of the film, and the obtained sample is marked as P-TC.</p>

<h3 id="sect306"><span class="b_heading">2.2 Materials characterization</span></h3>
<span>X-ray diffraction (XRD) data were collected on a Bruker D8 X-ray diffractor using Cu Kα radiation (<span class="italic">λ</span> = 1.5406 Å). The morphologies of the prepared nanofibers were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, SU8010, Hitachi, Japan) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-2100F, Japan). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed on a Multilab 2000 to investigate the valence states of the surface atoms. Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms (BET) were obtained on a Micromeritics ASAP 2460 instrument. Thermo-gravimetric measurement (TGA) was conducted on a STA 449 F3 Jupiter thermogravimetric analyzer (Netzsch, German).</span>

<h3 id="sect311"><span class="b_heading">2.3 Electrochemical characterization</span></h3>
<span>For electrochemical measurements, the TC or P-TC nanofibers were milled into powders and made into electrodes. The electrodes were composed of nanofibers, super P and PVDF at a weight ratio of 8<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1. Cu foil was used as current collector, and the diameter of each electrode is 12 mm. The average mass loading is ∼1 mg cm<small><sup>−2</sup></small>. CR2032 coin cells were assembled in a glovebox filled with high purity argon atmosphere (O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O &lt; 0.05 ppm). A sodium foil was used as both counter and reference electrode, and glass fiber (Whatman) was used as the separator. The electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in propylene carbonate (PC) and ethylene carbonate (EC) with a volume ratio of 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1. All the cells were cycled using a Land automatic battery tester. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were measured on a Gamry Reference 3000 electrochemical workstation at the open circuit potential in the frequency range from 1 MHz to 0.01 Hz.</span>


<h2 id="sect319"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
<span>The XRD patterns of TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C (TC) and Plasma-TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C (P-TC) nanofibers are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a>. The main XRD peaks of each sample can be indexed to the (101), (200), (105), (211), and (204) planes, corresponding to anatase TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> (JCPDS no. 021-1272). Besides, a bump at around 22° can also be observed, and it is related to the carbon fibers.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> The TGA results of the TC and P-TC sample are shown in Fig. S1.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> It can be seen that the carbon contents in all two samples are about 18.0% and 18.6%, respectively.</span>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c9ra03224j-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">XRD patterns of the TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C (TC) and Plasma-TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C (P-TC) samples.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">The morphology and microstructure of the two samples are demonstrated <span class="italic">via</span> SEM. SEM images in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a and b</a> show that TC and P-TC nanofibers all display a connected network-like structure, with a uniform diameter distribution of about 400 nm. However, the slight changes can be observed from their morphologies at a high magnification in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c and d</a>. After plasma treatment, surfaces of the fibers became rougher, and this may be the result of the plasma etching.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c9ra03224j-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">SEM images of the TC and P-TC samples at low magnification (a and b) and high magnification (c and d).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">The results of Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area and pore size distribution measured by N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption–desorption isotherms are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3</a>. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>, the surface area of TC is determined to be 89 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. After the plasma treatment, the surface area changes to 118 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>. This means the surface of fibers have been corroded by the plasma and the area was increased by 33%. It can be seen from <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c–d</a> that the pore size distribution of the two are similar, with the pore size mainly between 10 and 40 nm. But the content of pores with the size around 10 nm increases slightly after the plasma treatment.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c9ra03224j-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption–desorption isotherms of TC (a) and P-TC (b); the corresponding BJH pore size distribution curves of TC (c) and P-TC (d).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">Considering that the plasma may react with the carbon fibers, XPS is employed to investigate the surface compositions and chemical states of TC and P-TC nanofibers. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a> shows the XPS spectra of Ti 2p. In both samples, two peaks located at around 458.2 and 463.9 eV can be observed, and these two peaks both correspond to Ti<small><sup>4+</sup></small> in TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>. No obvious differences are observed in these two spectra, which means that the air plasma treatments do not change the chemical states of Ti. For other three elements, there have been some changes in the spectra, and the details are presented below. The C 1s spectra are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>. In the spectra of TC, only one peak appears at 284.8 eV, and it is related to the C–C bonds. However, two extra peaks at around 286 and 288.5 eV appear after the air plasma treatments, which may be corresponding to the C–O–C and O–C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O bonds.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> These results imply that during the plasma treatments, the oxygen in the air may have reacted with the carbon fibers, resulting in the generation of new oxygen-containing functional groups, such as C–O and C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O. The reaction mechanism was explained by Liu's work that the bombardment of the plasma caused the breaking of some C–C bonds and then generated edge-dangling bonds. Meanwhile, the edge-dangling bonds reacted with oxygen to form oxygen-containing functional groups.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> Wang <span class="italic">et al.</span> reported that O-doped architecture of graphite domain can promote the electrical conductivity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> Similar stories may happen here, the C–O and C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O bonds introduced by the plasma treatments may also increase the electrical conductivity. Consistent results can be obtained based on the O 1s spectra shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a>. Before the plasma treatment, a peak at 529.8 eV and a shoulder at 531.4 eV can be observed. The previous peak is related to the Ti–O bond, and it may be assigned to the lattice oxygen. The latter shoulder is ascribed to the formation of oxygen vacancies.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41,42</span></sup></a> This shoulder may be ascribed to that the crystal sizes of the TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> we obtain are too small, and there are many vacancies on the surfaces.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a> After the plasma treatments, a peak at 533.1 eV appears in the spectra, and it corresponds to the C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O bond. This result also implies that oxygen in the air may have reacted with the carbon during the plasma treatments. The electronegativity of oxygen is very high, and oxygen-containing functional groups attract electrons from the surrounding carbon atoms, resulting in the rearrangement of the surrounding charges. Carbon atoms with positive charge are active sites, thereby enhancing the electrochemical properties of carbon materials. The N 1s spectra for the TC and P-TC samples are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d</a>. For TC, a small peak appears at 398.4 eV, which may be related to the pyridine-like N atoms (N-6) from raw material.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a> The P-TC spectrum has two peaks at about 399.8 and 401.1 eV. These two peaks are related to the pyrrolic N (N-5) and graphitic N (N-Q).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> All these results indicate that after the plasma treatment, the TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> kept unchanged, but new functional groups, edge-dangling bonds and even defects on carbon fibers were introduced, which made the carbon fibers have a better electrical conductivity.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c9ra03224j-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">High-resolution XPS spectra of Ti 2p (a), C 1s (b), O 1s (c) and N 1s (d) of the TC sample and P-TC samples.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">In order to understand the microstructures of the nanofibers after plasma treatment, both samples were observed <span class="italic">via</span> transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The results of TEM images, high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns of the two samples are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5</a>. It can be seen that the surface of P-TC sample (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a and b</a>) was obviously rougher than the TC sample (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e and f</a>), which infer the surface may have been corroded by the plasma. In both samples, TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoparticles piled along the fiber orientation and the diameters of these particles are several nanometers. The lattice fringes in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a>) has a spacing of 0.35 nm, corresponding to the (101) planes of anatase TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>. All <span class="italic">d</span>-spacings derived from the SAED pattern (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a>) can be assigned to TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>. As to the P-TC sample, similar information is obtained (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5g and h</a>). These results confirmed again that after the plasma treatments, TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> in the fibers kept unchanged.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c9ra03224j-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">TEM (a and b), HRTEM (c), and SAED result (d) of the P-TC sample; TEM (e and f), HRTEM (g), and SAED result (h) of the TC sample.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">The electrochemical performances of two samples were evaluated in sodium half cells. The galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles at 0.2C are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>. For two samples, the irreversible capacities in the initial cycles result from the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and the decomposition of electrolyte. After the first cycle, all the electrodes display sloped charge–discharge profiles without distinct plateaus, suggesting the Na insertion into the TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> lattice is homogeneous. The voltage profiles in the second and third cycles overlap for both of electrodes, which demonstrates the insertion and extraction processes are reversible. During 100 cycles, the capacities of TC and P-TC samples are both about 250 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. According to the TGA results, the TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> contents in both samples are larger than 80%, which infers the main capacities of the two samples came from TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Since TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> kept unchanged during the plasma treatments, the overall capacities before and after plasma treatments at a low rate of 0.2C kept nearly unchanged. To provide further evidence for the structural stability of the plasma treated TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers, SEM images of the P-TC nanofibers were taken from the cycled electrode (shown in Fig. S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). It can be seen from Fig. S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> that the fibrous morphology of the plasma treated TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers remains intact after 3 and 60 cycles. Moreover, there seems to be a thick film in the latter sample, which may be the grown SEI films after 60 cycles. This result indicates a strong tolerance of the material to the volumetric changes during Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-insertion/extraction cycles.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c9ra03224j-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">Charge/discharge curves measured at a current of 66 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (0.2C) of TC (a) and P-TC (b); cycling performances of the two samples (c).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">However, at high current densities, the two samples show distinguished performances. It should be mentioned that all batteries in our work are cycled at 0.5C for 3 cycles before high-rate tests. As displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7</a>, when the current density is 5C (1650 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), the capacities for TC and P-TC after 500 cycles are 93 and 204 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. The corresponding capacity retention ratios are 79.9% and 92.8%. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7b</a>, when the electrodes are cycled at 10C (3300 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), the two samples could maintain capacities of 81 and 191 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. The capacity retention ratios after 500 cycles are 85% and 94%. These results show that the plasma treatment greatly improved the rate capability and cycling performance at high rates. The rate performances are demonstrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7c</a>. It can be seen that the P-TC electrode can deliver much larger capacities at high rates. Even at 10C, the reversible capacity of 200 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> could be obtained. When the current density goes back to 0.2C, the charge capacity could be maintained at around 260 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, which exhibit a superior cycling performance.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c9ra03224j-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">Cycling performances of the two samples at a rate of 5C (a), 10C (b); rate capability at different rates from 0.2C to 10C (c).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">In order to understand why the two samples show different rate performances, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy experiments were carried out to study their resistances. For each sample, the data was taken from the same battery cycled after 1<small><sup>st</sup></small> and 50<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle at a rate of 0.5C. The equivalent circuit for fitting Nyquist plots is shown in the inset picture of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8a</a>. It can be seen that after the first cycle, the charge transfer resistance of the P-TC sample (169 Ω) is significantly smaller than the TC sample (586 Ω), which means plasma treatment may have increased the electrical conductivity. As discussed above, the surface area of P-TC sample is only 33% larger than TC sample, thus it is inferred that the conductivity of the fibers after plasma treatment is greatly improved. After 50 cycles, the resistance of TC sample increases to 632 Ω. However, the resistance of P-TC becomes smaller, which is 153 Ω. After cycled for 100 cycles, the resistance of P-TC sample is still smaller than the TC sample. These results indicate that the P-TC sample keeps stable during the electrochemical cycles. As discussed above, during the plasma treatments, TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> kept unchanged while the C fibers have reacted with the plasma. And the changes of carbon fibers might increase the total electrical conductivity and then decrease the charge transfer resistance of the overall material. However, the detailed mechanism still needs further investigation.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig8"><a href="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f8_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c9ra03224j-f8.tif" src="/image/article/2019/RA/c9ra03224j/c9ra03224j-f8.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 8 </b> <span id="fig8"><span class="graphic_title">EIS results of TC (a) and P-TC (b); the inset shows the equivalent circuit used for simulating.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>

<h2 id="sect420"><span class="a_heading">4. Conclusions</span></h2>
<span>In this work, TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanofibers are prepared by an electrospinning method, and the air plasma treatments are employed to modify the fibers. Electrochemical results show the rate performances of the samples are greatly improved upon the plasma treatments. Detailed studies indicate that the plasma have reacted with the C fibers and the surface areas are increased. These effects make the samples have a higher electronic conductivity and cyclability during continuous discharge/charge cycles. Our results suggest plasma treatments may be an effective method to modify the carbon based electrode materials and make them present better electrochemical performances.</span>

Ji, Hierarchical Hollow-Microsphere Metal-Selenide@Carbon Composites with Rational Surface Engineering for Advanced Sodium Storage, <span class="italic">Adv. Energy Mater.</span>, 2019, <span class="bold">9</span>, 1803035&nbsp;<a target="_blank" class="DOILink" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/aenm.201803035" title="DOI Link to resource 10.1002/aenm.201803035">CrossRef</a>&nbsp;<a target="_blank" class="URLLink" href="/en/Content/OpenURL/50001067/c9ra03224j/10_1" title="Link via OpenURL Resolver"><img height="14px" alt="" src="/en/Image/GetOpenURLImage/50001067"></a>.</span></li><li><span id="cit11">M.
